    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Sequencing a Juglans regia &#8201;&#215;&#8201; J. microcarpa hybrid yields high-quality genome assemblies of parental species"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2019 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-03-25"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Members of the genus Juglans are monecious wind-pollinated trees in the family Juglandaceae with highly heterozygous genomes, which greatly complicates genome sequence assembly. The genomes of interspecific hybrids are usually comprised of haploid genomes of parental species. We exploited this attribute of interspecific hybrids to avoid heterozygosity and sequenced an interspecific hybrid Juglans microcarpa&#8201;&#215;&#8201;J. regia using a novel combination of single-molecule sequencing and optical genome mapping technologies. The resulting assemblies of both genomes were remarkably complete including chromosome termini and centromere regions. Chromosome termini consisted of arrays of telomeric repeats about 8&#8201;kb long and heterochromatic subtelomeric regions about 10&#8201;kb long. The centromeres consisted of arrays of a centromere-specific Gypsy retrotransposon and most contained genes, many of them transcribed. Juglans genomes evolved by a whole-genome-duplication dating back to the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary and consist of two subgenomes, which were fractionated by numerous short gene deletions evenly distributed along the length of the chromosomes. Fractionation was shown to be asymmetric with one subgenome exhibiting greater gene loss than the other. The asymmetry of the process is ongoing and mirrors an asymmetry in gene expression between the subgenomes. Given the importance of J. microcarpa&#8201;&#215;&#8201;J. regia hybrids as potential walnut rootstocks, we catalogued disease resistance genes in the parental genomes and studied their chromosomal distribution. We also estimated the molecular clock rates for woody perennials and deployed them in estimating divergence times of Juglans genomes and those of other woody perennials. The genetic sequence of a hybrid walnut tree sheds light on the evolution of the parental species and on some important traits. A study led by Ming-Cheng Luo and&#160;Jan Dvorak at the University of California Davis, USA, describes a new approach for producing high-quality genome assemblies of the parental species from interspecific hybrids. By applying long-read sequencing technology and optical mapping to a popular hybrid between cultivated Persian/English walnut and a wild relative that is native to North America they were able to completely assemble the genomes of these two species, gain insights into the evolution of their chromosomes and the distribution of disease-resistant genes. This approach could be used on other outcrossing&#160; crops to generate reference-quality genome assemblies&#160;and accelerate genetic improvements in commercially important crops."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://www.nature.com/articles/s41438-019-0139-1"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Horticulture Research"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Sequencing a Juglans regia × J. microcarpa hybrid yields high-quality genome assemblies of parental species"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="The genetic sequence of a hybrid walnut tree sheds light on the evolution of the parental species and on some important traits. A study led by Ming-Cheng Luo and&amp;nbsp;Jan Dvorak at the University of California Davis, USA, describes a new approach for producing high-quality genome assemblies of the parental species from interspecific hybrids. By applying long-read sequencing technology and optical mapping to a popular hybrid between cultivated Persian/English walnut and a wild relative that is native to North America they were able to completely assemble the genomes of these two species, gain insights into the evolution of their chromosomes and the distribution of disease-resistant genes. This approach could be used on other outcrossing&amp;nbsp; crops to generate reference-quality genome assemblies&amp;nbsp;and accelerate genetic improvements in commercially important crops."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/m685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig1_HTML.png"/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-5972-7963"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-5972-7963</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="USDA-ARS" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0404 0958, grid.463419.d, Crops Pathology and Genetics Research Unit, USDA-ARS, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Patrick_J_-Brown" data-author-popup="auth-Patrick_J_-Brown">Patrick J. Brown</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Charles_A_-Leslie" data-author-popup="auth-Charles_A_-Leslie">Charles A. Leslie</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Mallikarjuna_K_-Aradhya" data-author-popup="auth-Mallikarjuna_K_-Aradhya">Mallikarjuna K. Aradhya</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="USDA-ARS" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0404 0958, grid.463419.d, National Clonal Germplasm Repository, USDA-ARS, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Abhaya_M_-Dandekar" data-author-popup="auth-Abhaya_M_-Dandekar">Abhaya M. Dandekar</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7925-4086"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-7925-4086</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Patrick_E_-McGuire" data-author-popup="auth-Patrick_E_-McGuire">Patrick E. McGuire</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Daniel-Kluepfel" data-author-popup="auth-Daniel-Kluepfel">Daniel Kluepfel</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-1116-0064"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-1116-0064</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="USDA-ARS" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0404 0958, grid.463419.d, Crops Pathology and Genetics Research Unit, USDA-ARS, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jan-Dvorak" data-author-popup="auth-Jan-Dvorak" data-corresp-id="c1">Jan Dvorak<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Ming_Cheng-Luo" data-author-popup="auth-Ming_Cheng-Luo" data-corresp-id="c2">Ming-Cheng Luo<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-9744-5887"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-9744-5887</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California, Davis, CA, 95616, USA" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Members of the genus <i>Juglans</i> are monecious wind-pollinated trees in the family Juglandaceae with highly heterozygous genomes, which greatly complicates genome sequence assembly. The genomes of interspecific hybrids are usually comprised of haploid genomes of parental species. We exploited this attribute of interspecific hybrids to avoid heterozygosity and sequenced an interspecific hybrid <i>Juglans microcarpa</i> × <i>J. regia</i> using a novel combination of single-molecule sequencing and optical genome mapping technologies. The resulting assemblies of both genomes were remarkably complete including chromosome termini and centromere regions. Chromosome termini consisted of arrays of telomeric repeats about 8 kb long and heterochromatic subtelomeric regions about 10 kb long. The centromeres consisted of arrays of a centromere-specific <i>Gypsy</i> retrotransposon and most contained genes, many of them transcribed. <i>Juglans</i> genomes evolved by a whole-genome-duplication dating back to the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary and consist of two subgenomes, which were fractionated by numerous short gene deletions evenly distributed along the length of the chromosomes. Fractionation was shown to be asymmetric with one subgenome exhibiting greater gene loss than the other. The asymmetry of the process is ongoing and mirrors an asymmetry in gene expression between the subgenomes. Given the importance of <i>J. microcarpa</i> × <i>J. regia</i> hybrids as potential walnut rootstocks, we catalogued disease resistance genes in the parental genomes and studied their chromosomal distribution. We also estimated the molecular clock rates for woody perennials and deployed them in estimating divergence times of <i>Juglans</i> genomes and those of other woody perennials.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Haplotype phasing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zhang, F. et al. Haplotype phasing of whole human genomes using bead-based barcode partitioning in a single tube. Nat. Biotechnol. 35, 852–857 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d27278e664">1</a></sup> and navigating between allelic and nonallelic variation are the major challenges in assembling genomes of outcrossing species with high levels of heterozygosity such as found in members of the genus <i>Juglans</i>. Genome sequencing targeting outcrossing plants employed inbred lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d27278e671">2</a></sup>, haploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d27278e675">3</a></sup>, and megagametophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Neale, D. B., Martínez-García, P. J., De La Torre, A. R., Montanari, S. &amp; Wei, X. X. Tree genome sequencing: Novel insights into plant biology. Ann. Rev. Plant Biol. 68, 457–483 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d27278e679">4</a></sup> to avoid heterozygosity.</p><p>Interspecific hybrids offer another strategy to avoid heterozygosity. Since the genome of an interspecific hybrid is usually comprised of haploid genomes of the parental species, interspecific hybrids have the same advantages for genome sequencing as haploids, but are usually easy to produce. Technical difficulties with allocating scaffolds to parental genomes have precluded the deployment of hybrids in genome sequencing. Using an interspecific hybrid between cultivated walnut and its wild relative <i>J. microcarpa</i>, we describe here a novel approach to sequencing hybrid genomes which results in a cost-effective high-quality genome assembly for both parents.</p><p>The cultivated Persian/English walnut, <i>Juglans regia</i>, is native to Asia whereas <i>J. microcarpa</i> is native to North America, where it occurs in riparian areas in the southwestern USA. Both species are wind-pollinated, highly heterozygous, and intolerant of inbreeding. Their hybrids are infertile. Both have a genome size of about 600 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e698">5</a></sup> with <i>n</i> = 16.</p><p>English walnut is an important nut crop with 3.8 million tons harvested worldwide in 2017 (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#data/QC">http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#data/QC</a>). Walnut production has been steadily increasing in part due to health benefits derived from including walnuts in the human diet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pan, A., Sun, Q., Manson, J. E., Willett, W. C. &amp; Hu, F. B. Walnut consumption is associated with lower risk of type 2 diabetes in women. J. Nutr. 143, 512–518 (2013)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d27278e715">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kris-Etherton, P. M. Walnuts decrease risk of cardiovascular disease: a summary of efficacy and biologic mechanisms. J. Nutr. 144, 547s–554s (2014)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d27278e715_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Poulose, S. M., Miller, M. G. &amp; Shukitt-Hale, B. Role of walnuts in maintaining brain health with age. J. Nutr. 144, 561s–566s (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d27278e718">8</a></sup>. In the USA, English walnut trees are grown commercially using rootstocks chosen for their ability to tolerate such soil-borne pathogens as <i>Phytophthora</i> spp.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Browne, G. T. et al. Resistance to Species of Phytophthora identified among clones of Juglans microcarpa x J. regia. Hortsci 50, 1136–1142 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d27278e725">9</a></sup>, lesion nematodes, and <i>Agrobacterium tumefaciens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kluepfel, D. A. et al. Evaluation of wild walnut Juglans spp. for resistance to crown gall disease. Phytopathology 101, S92–S92 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27278e731">10</a></sup>, which are all serious pathogens of walnut. The commercial hybrid rootstock <i>J. microcarpa</i> × <i>J. regia</i> possessing tolerance to soil borne diseases is extensively used in walnut production in California. The development of genomic resources for walnut and its wild relatives, including reference-quality genome sequences, will accelerate genetic improvement of walnut scions and rootstocks.</p><p><i>Juglans</i> and its relatives in the family Juglandaceae are members of order Fagales, which includes many important forest trees. Reference-quality genome sequences will facilitate comparative genomics and will advance biology of this important group of woody perennials. Recent attempts to sequence the heterozygous genome of English walnut using traditional approaches resulted in assemblies with 4402 scaffolds with N50 = 640 kb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e748">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e751">11</a></sup> and 25,670 scaffolds with N50 = 310 kb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Bai, W. N. et al. Demographically idiosyncratic responses to climate change and rapid Pleistocene diversification of the walnut genus Juglans (Juglandaceae) revealed by whole-genome sequences. New Phytol. 217, 1726–1736 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27278e755">12</a></sup>. An attempt to sequence the heterozygous genome of <i>J. microcarpa</i> resulted in an even more fragmented assembly with 329,873 scaffolds with N50 = 136 kb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e762">5</a></sup>. The novel sequencing approach described here exploits the synergy between long-read sequencing and optical genome mapping. The average length of reads produced with long-read platforms exceeds the lengths of a vast majority of plant long-terminal-repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), which results in a dramatically improved sequence assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. Hybrid assembly of the large and highly repetitive genome of Aegilops tauschii, a progenitor of bread wheat, with the MaSuRCA mega-reads algorithm. Genome Res. 27, 787–792 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d27278e766">13</a></sup>. Contigs or scaffolds assembled from long-reads are sufficiently long to be aligned on genome-wide optical maps, which can be assembled with very high accuracy, even for large or polyploid plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498–502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d27278e771">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Dvorak, J. et al. Structural variation and rates of genome evolution in the grass family seen through comparison of sequences of genomes greatly differing in size. Plant J. 95, 487–503 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d27278e774">15</a></sup>. Alignments of the optical maps of a hybrid onto the optical maps of its parents will assign contigs to parental genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zhu, T. et al. Analysis of Brachypodium genomes with genome-wide optical maps. Genome 61, 559–561 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d27278e778">16</a></sup>, while also serving as an assembly quality control<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498–502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d27278e782">14</a></sup>.</p><p>We used the assembled <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> genome sequences in conjunction with the Juglandoid whole-genome-duplication (WGD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Synteny analysis in Rosids with a walnut physical map reveals slow genome evolution in long-lived woody perennials. BMC Genom. 16, 707 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27278e795">17</a></sup>, and the Juglandaceae fossil record to calibrate the molecular clock rate for woody perennials. We used the calibrated molecular clock to estimate the time of divergence of <i>Juglans</i> species and other woody perennials. Based on synteny within <i>Juglans</i> genomes, we allocated the 16 <i>Juglans</i> chromosomes produced by the Juglandoid WGD into eight homoeologous chromosome pairs and analyzed their evolution. Finally, we exploited the contiguity of the assemblies in the analyses of the structure and evolution of <i>Juglans</i> telomeres and centromeres and the distribution of disease resistance genes in the <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> genomes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>We sequenced 58 Pacific Biosciences SMRT cells of the hybrid (MS1-56) <i>J. microcarpa</i> 31.01 (henceforth 31.01) × <i>J. regia</i> cv Serr (henceforth Serr) and assembled the reads (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S1</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S1</a>) into 460 polished contigs (for a flowchart of the entire assembly see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S2</a>). The total length of this assembly (MS1-56_v0) was 1,056,053,408 bp (N50 = 7,963,037 bp) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S2</a>).</p><p>Next, we constructed two optical maps for the hybrid and one for each of its parents (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S3</a>). The N50 of the optical contigs ranged from 1.31 to 2.90 Mb. The parental maps consisted of ‘haploid’ regions, in which the haplotypes were similar enough to collapse into a single contig, and ‘diploid’ regions, in which the haplotypes were dissimilar enough to be assembled into separate contigs (phased). The haploid and diploid regions were identified by a map self-alignment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S3</a>). The mean length across the phased regions was 108 Mb (16.8%) in Serr and 33 Mb (5.9%) in 31.01 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S4</a>). Due to the inclusion of phased haplotypes in a map, the sum of the total lengths of the Serr and 31.01 optical maps was 13.4% longer than the length of the MS1-56 optical map (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><p>To ascertain whether the genome of our hybrid was complete, we compared the total length of its optical map to the sum of the lengths of the optical maps of the parents, which we edited to disregard the redundant haplotypes from the phased regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S3</a>). The total length of the edited maps of Serr and 31.01 differed from the length of the map of MS1-56 by only 1 Mb (0.09%) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S4</a>). We therefore concluded that the hybrid genome was a complete representation of the parental genomes.</p><p>We aligned the sequence contigs on the optical map of the hybrid, stitched them into scaffolds (assembly MS1-56_scf, total length = 1,066,408,726 bp, N50 = 34,776,948 bp), and allocated 40 of them comprising 99.85% of the hybrid genome assembly into the parental genomes with the aid of the optical maps of the parents (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S3</a>). The remaining 224 scaffolds representing 0.15% of the hybrid genome sequence were too short (mean = 7.2 kb) to be aligned on an optical map and were aligned on Illumina sequence of the <i>J. regia</i> cv Chandler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e882">11</a></sup> for assigning to parental genomes.</p><p>Finally, we ordered and oriented scaffolds on high-density genetic maps producing 16 pseudomolecules for each of the two genomes. They had only five gaps of unknown lengths (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S4</a>); the remaining gaps were estimated based on the optical map alignments. We then reduced gap lengths or closed them entirely with unassigned contigs or contigs produced with the 10X Genomics technology (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S1</a>).</p><p>We mapped 89X Illumina reads of the hybrid to the genome assemblies and detected 48,717 and 51,789 indels and 748 and 1,205 base substitutions in the Serr and 31.01 assemblies, respectively (about one error per 10,000 bp). We subsequently corrected these errors and produced the final assemblies JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Transposable element and gene annotation</h3><p>Transposable elements (TEs) represented 41.02 and 40.86% of the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S6</a>). The density of the <i>Copia</i> LTR-RTs decreased and that of the <i>Gypsy</i> LTR-RTs increased from the chromosome ends towards the centromeres (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig1">1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S5</a>). The JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies each contained 0.03% satellite DNA and 351,359 and 349,951 simple sequence repeats (SSRs), respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S7</a>). SSR density was highest in the distal regions of the pseudomolecules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Structure and phylogeny of Juglans genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Structure and phylogeny of <i>Juglans</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="359" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Circular plot of the <i>Juglans regia</i> pseudomolecules. Circles from outside to inside are: pseudomolecule coordinates in Mb, density of all types of LTR-RTs, density of <i>Gypsy</i> LTR-RTs, density of <i>Copia</i> LTR-RTs, and gene density. Central lines connect syntenic blocks across chromosomes. Different colors represent different homoeologous chromosome pairs derived from the Juglandoid WGD. <b>b</b> Dot-plot alignments of the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules against Jm31.01_v1.0 pseudomolecules. The starts of the pseudomolecules are to the left or bottom. The blue diagonal across the plots of the 16 chromosomes indicate collinearity of homoeologous chromosomes between the two genomes. The green dots indicate homoeologous chromosomes within a genome, which originated by the Juglandoid WGD. The short black and red dot traces indicate paralogues produced the γ whole genome triplication</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We annotated 31,425 and 29,496 high-confidence (HC) protein-coding genes in the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies<b>;</b> 31,286 and 29,397 were on the pseudomolecules and 139 and 99 were on unassigned scaffolds (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S9</a>). Of 1,440 BUSCO v3 genes, 96.0 and 95.2% were among the HC genes annotated in the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies, which is comparable to coverage reported in other genome annotations in plants (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S10</a>). Serr and 31.01 genes were on average 4,226 and 4,391 bp long and had 5.44 and 5.60 exons averaging 258.64 and 249.38 bp (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S8</a>). Transcription factors were encoded by 2,046 (6.5%) and 2,032 (6.9%) genes in the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S11</a>). There were 18,772 (59.7%) and 18,891 (64.0%) single-copy genes and 744 and 625 multigene families in the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies. The larger number of genes annotated in the <i>J. regia</i> genome compared to the <i>J. microcarpa</i> genome was due to the larger number of duplicated genes in the former.</p><p>Average gene density was one gene per 16.9 and 17.7 kb in the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 pseudomolecules. Gene density decreased and TE density increased from pseudomolecule ends to centromeric regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig1">1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S5</a>), and the two variables were negatively correlated (<i>r</i> = −0.82, <i>p</i> &lt; 0.01). Gene density was positively correlated with meiotic recombination rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Synteny analysis in Rosids with a walnut physical map reveals slow genome evolution in long-lived woody perennials. BMC Genom. 16, 707 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1013">17</a></sup> along the <i>J. regia</i> pseudomolecules (<i>r</i> = 0.78, <i>p</i> &lt; 0.01).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">
                           <i>Juglans</i> genome structure and evolution</h3><p>Of the genes annotated on the Serr pseudomolecules, 26,403 (84.4%) were collinear with genes on the <i>J. microcarpa</i> pseudomolecules (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM2">S1</a>). The two genomes differed by 28 inversions involving &gt; 3 collinear genes, 21 segmental duplications, 3 intra- and 14 inter-chromosomal interstitial translocations, but no terminal translocation (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM2">S1</a>, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig1">1b</a>). Only 90 (0.28%) of the JrSerr_v1.0 genes were not detected in the Jm31.01_v1.0 assembly.</p><p>We computed <i>Ks</i> divergence among the <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> genes to analyze gene duplication and divergence. The <i>Ks</i> plot showed three peaks (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2a</a>). The first peak mostly consisted of <i>Ks</i> values between orthologous genes in the two genomes and reflected their divergence. The second peak coincided with the major <i>Ks</i> peak in self-searches within the Serr and 31.01 genomes and reflected the divergence of paralogous genes, which originated by the Juglandoid WGD. The third peak coincided with the major peak in self-searches within the grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>) genome and reflected divergence between genes duplicated by the whole genome triplication (γWGT) first described in the grape genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1079">18</a></sup>. These inferences were confirmed by self-alignment plots (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig1">1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S5</a>, File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM2">S1</a>), a plot of the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules against the grape pseudomolecules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S7</a>), and a plot against the <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> scaffolds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Albert, V. A. et al. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1467 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1102">19</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Whole-genome duplication and triplication in the Juglans lineage."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Whole-genome duplication and triplication in the <i>Juglans</i> lineage.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="581" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Densities of <i>K</i>s divergence in the BLASTP homology searches of JrSerr_v1.0 against Jm31.01_v1.0, within JrSerr_v1.0, and within the grape genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1131">18</a></sup>. <b>b</b> Self-alignments of the <i>J. regia</i> Serr pseudomolecules. Pseudomolecules are oriented as in 1b. <b>c</b> Divergence of the genomes of <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa, Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Malus domestica</i>, <i>J. regia</i> cv Serr, and <i>J. microcarpa</i> acc. 31.01. The dendogram was constructed from <i>K</i>a divergence of 1155 single-copy genes. Bootstrap confidence on each node is shown. Also shown are estimated times of critical events in <i>Juglans</i> genome evolution, including the γWGT and the Juglandoid WGD (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S13</a>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>With the sole exception of <i>Rhoiptelea chiliantha</i>, all species of Juglandaceae share <i>n</i> = 16 or a chromosome number derived from it<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Manos, P. S. &amp; Stone, D. E. Evolution, phylogeny, and systematics of the Juglandaceae. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 88, 231–269 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1193">20</a></sup>. <i>Rhoiptelea chiliantha</i> and the neighboring family Myricaceae have <i>n</i> = 8; <i>n</i> = 16 was derived from <i>n</i> = 8 by the Juglandoid WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Synteny analysis in Rosids with a walnut physical map reveals slow genome evolution in long-lived woody perennials. BMC Genom. 16, 707 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1210">17</a></sup>. The fossil record showed that Juglandaceae appeared in the upper Cretaceous and the family radiated in the Paleocene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Manchester, S. R. The fossil history of Juglandaceae. Monographs In Systematic Botany from the Missouri Botanical Garden. 21, 1–137 (1987)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1214">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Manchester, S. R. Early history of the Juglandaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 162, 231–250 (1989)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1214_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, J. B. et al. Integrated fossil and molecular data reveal the biogeographic diversification of the Eastern Asian-Eastern North American Disjunct Hickory Genus (Carya Nutt.). PLoS. ONE. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0070449&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1217">23</a></sup>. The Juglandoid WGD must have therefore originated prior to the radiation of Juglandaceae in the Paleocene but after the divergence of <i>R. chiliantha</i> in the Cretaceous. We therefore chose the Cretaceous-Paleogene (C-Pg) boundary (66 MYA) as the approximate time of the origin of the Juglandoid WGD. Of the 31,286 genes on the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules, 12,195 (39.0%) were genes duplicated by the Juglandoid WGD that were in collinear locations on the homoeologous chromosomes. Using the WGD time estimate and the average <i>Ka</i> and <i>Ks</i> between these paralogues in the Serr and 31.01 genomes, we estimated clock rates as 5.6 × 10<sup>–10</sup> nucleotide substitutions year<sup>−1</sup> using <i>Ka</i> and 2.5 × 10<sup>–9</sup> nucleotide substitutions year<sup>−1</sup> using <i>Ks</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S12</a>).</p><p>To date the divergence of <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i>, we identified 1155 single-copy genes shared by <i>J. regia, J. microcarpa</i>, and six other sequenced genomes of woody perennials, including grape and <i>A. trichopoda</i>, and constructed a dendogram (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2c</a>). Its topology was consistent with branching of the tree of life (<a href="http://tolweb.org/Core_Eudicots/20714">http://tolweb.org/Core_Eudicots/20714</a>). However, the dating of <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> divergence, about 8 MYA, was one-half to one-quarter of previously reported estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1281">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Xiang, X. G. et al. Large-scale phylogenetic analyses reveal fagalean diversification promoted by the interplay of diaspores and environments in the Paleogene. Perspect. Plant Ecol. 16, 101–110 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1284">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Dong, W. P. et al. Phylogenetic resolution in juglans based on complete chloroplast genomes and nuclear DNA sequences. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1148 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1287">25</a></sup>. Our estimates of divergence time of the <i>Amborella</i> lineage, the grape lineage, and the origin of the γWGT (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S13</a>) would be more recent than the published data or fossil records<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. M. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. J. Establishing a time-scale for plant evolution. New Phytol. 192, 266–301 (2011)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1298">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, Y. N. et al. A genome triplication associated with early diversification of the core eudicots. Genome Biol. 13, R3 (2012)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1298_1">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zeng, L. P. et al. Resolution of deep eudicot phylogeny and their temporal diversification using nuclear genes from transcriptomic and genomic datasets. New Phytol. 214, 1338–1354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1301">28</a></sup> if this discrepancy were caused by an incorrectly calibrated molecular clock or incorrectly computed divergence times. However, this was not the case.</p><p>If we failed to correctly phase the <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> haplotypes during the assembly of the hybrid genome sequence, orthologous genes in our JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies would appear more closely related to each other than they actually are and the age of divergence of the two genomes could be underestimated. To test this possibility, we computed the <i>Ka</i> and <i>Ks</i> values and time of divergence between <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> using sequences of 1077 single-copy genes independently sequenced by Stevens et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1328">5</a></sup>. The <i>Ka</i> and <i>Ks</i> values we obtained were similar to those computed from our gene sequences (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S13</a>). We then constructed a chronogram including genomes of peach, apple, poplar, and cocoa and the six <i>Juglans</i> genomes reported by Stevens et al<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1345">5</a></sup>. in addition to the genomes used in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2c</a> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S9</a>). The topology of the chronogram was similar to that in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2c</a> and the divergence time estimates of the branches shared with Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2c</a> were within the 95% confidence intervals of estimates in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2c</a> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S13</a>). Based on these analyses, we suggest that the discrepancies in dating of the <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> divergence is due to the use of an unrealistic rate of the molecular clock in the previous studies.</p><p>We analyzed gene collinearity along homoeologous chromosomes within the Serr genome and detected 38 paracentric inversions involving &gt;3 collinear genes and 20 intra- and 16 inter-chromosomal interstitial translocations. The homoeologues did not differ by any terminal translocations and retained a 1:1 relationship. Using the grape genome as an outgroup, we assigned rearrangements to phylogenetic branches (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM2">S1</a>) and computed the rates of their accumulation. The rates ranged from 0.4 to 1.4 major rearrangements per MY (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S14</a>).</p><p>The collinearity analysis showed that the 16 <i>J. regia</i> chromosomes can be built from 142 major synteny blocks making up the 19 grape chromosomes. Of them, 122 (85.9%) were shared by the <i>J. regia</i> homoeologous chromosomes, and were in the same order along the them (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM2">S1</a> and Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S15</a>). These rearrangements must have taken place prior to the Juglandoid WGD. We found 43.7% of the <i>J. regia</i> genes collinear with genes on the grape pseudomolecules (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Asymmetric (biased) fractionation of the Juglandoid WGD</h3><p>In each pair of homoeologous chromosomes within a <i>Juglans</i> genome, one chromosome contained more genes than the other (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig3">3a</a>). We denoted the homoeologues with more genes as “dominant” and those with fewer genes as “subdominant,” allocated the 16 <i>Juglans</i> chromosomes into eight homoeologous chromosome pairs, which we arranged in descending order based on the number of genes in the dominant chromosome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig3">3a</a>), and renamed the chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S17</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Asymmetric gene fractionation of the Juglandoid WGD."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Asymmetric gene fractionation of the Juglandoid WGD.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="318" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Numbers of genes annotated on the JrSerr_v1.0 dominant and subdominant pseudomolecules. Numbers on the horizontal axis are designations of individual <i>Juglans</i> homoeologous groups, each consisting of one dominant (D) and one subdominant (S) chromosome. <b>b</b> Numbers of singleton genes in intervals delimited by conserved paralogues on the dominant chromosome Jr1D and subdominant homoeologues Jr1S. The homoeologous pseudomolecules Jr1D and Jr1S were subdivided into intervals delimited by successive pairs of paralogous genes in collinear locations on the homoeologues. The noncollinear genes (singletons) were counted in each interval (histogram bars) in the dominant pseudomolecule (blue) and subdominant pseudomolecule (red). The starting nucleotides of the pseudomolecules are to the left. The red arrow indicates the location of the centromere in the dominant pseudomolecules. <b>c</b> Numbers of genes on the pseudomolecule of the dominant chromosome Jr1D (blue) and subdominant chromosome Jr1S (red) showing dominant expression (two-fold higher expression than that of the orthologous gene) in 22 different <i>Juglans regia</i> RNAseq datasets. The description of the RNAseq datasets (coded at the horizontal axis of the graph) is in Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">12</a> and Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S20</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>There were 18,179 and 17,093 genes in the dominant subgenome and 13,107 and 12,304 genes in the subdominant subgenome in JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S18</a>). Both dominant and subdominant subgenomes had fewer genes than are in the <i>A. trichopoda</i> genome (26,846 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Albert, V. A. et al. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1467 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1478">19</a></sup>, which was not subject to a recent WGD (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig2">2c</a>). This comparison indicates that both dominant and subdominant <i>Juglans</i> subgenomes have lost genes since the WGD.</p><p>The difference in gene loss between dominant and subdominant homoeologues was nearly constant among the homoeologous pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig3">3a</a>), suggesting that the rate of gene loss was intrinsic to a subgenome. The rate of gene loss was also nearly constant along each chromosome. We subdivided homoeologous chromosomes into sections delineated by successive pairs of collinear genes and counted the numbers of singleton genes in the intervening intervals (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S19</a>). In the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules, 13,791 singletons were on the dominant chromosomes (mean of 3.2 per interval) but only 8,261 were on the subdominant chromosomes (mean of 1.9 per interval, <i>p</i> = 0.0001, paired <i>t</i>-test, <i>N</i> = 16). The numbers of singletons per interval varied little along each chromosome, except for the proximal regions, indicating that gene loss was uniform along the chromosomes and occurred by many deletions involving one or few genes along a chromosome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig3">3b</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">10</a>). In each chromosome, deletions were larger and more frequent in the proximal region than the rest of the chromosome, as the numbers of singletons per interval were greatly elevated in proximal regions. We also expressed the numbers of singletons per 2-Mb non-overlapping windows. Most of the windows in subdominant chromosomes contained fewer singletons than those in the dominant homoeologues (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><p>Since the divergence of the <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> lineages 8 MYA, 28.7% of singletons were lost from the subdominant chromosomes in <i>J. microcarpa</i> but only 14.9% from the corresponding intervals in the dominant homoeologues (<i>p</i> = 0.002, two-tailed paired <i>t</i>-test). Thus, the factor that caused the asymmetry in fractionation has persisted in the subgenomes since their origin to the recent past. In 22 RNAseq datasets (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S20</a>), genes on the dominant chromosomes were on average transcribed significantly more than their paralogues on the subdominant chromosomes (paired <i>t</i>-test, <i>p</i> <i>&lt;</i> 1.0 × 10<sup>–8</sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig3">3c</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">12</a>, File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM3">S2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">rRNA genes</h3><p>We failed to assemble the sequences of the arrays of 18S-5.8S-26S rRNA genes, although we were able to assemble an optical map of the locus on Jr4S (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">13</a>). The locus was 1,310,076 bp long (16,646,858–17,956,934 bp) and contained 112 gene units repeated in tandem; only on unit was present in the pseudomolecule.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Telomeres</h3><p>Complete arrays of the telomeric repeat CCCTAAA were present at 16 <i>J. regia</i> and 11 <i>J. microcarpa</i> termini (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S21</a>). These termini had a tripartite structure (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig4">4</a>). The telomeric regions consisted of monotonous arrays of telomeric repeats, with a sole exception of Jm6S which included a 47-bp (T)<sub>n</sub> SSR (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM4">S3</a>). The subtelomeric region spanned the distance from the last telomeric repeat to the first gene and had the characteristics of heterochromatin by consisting of various microsatellites interspersed with TEs. Juxtaposed to the subtelomeric region was the euchromatic region containing genes. Spacing of genes was estimated for the three most distal genes and was similar to the genome average (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Structure of chromosome termini in the Juglans regia and J. microcarpa pseudomolecules."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Structure of chromosome termini in the <i>Juglans regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> pseudomolecules.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="242" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The telomeric region consists entirely of telomeric repeats. The subtelomeric region consists of satellite motifs and TEs. The gene region starts with the coding sequence of the first gene. Mean intergenic distances and 95% confidence intervals (<i>N</i> = 16 and 11 for <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i>, respectively) were computed for the first three protein-coding genes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We anticipated finding a transitional zone containing degraded telomeric repeats near the boundaries between the telomeric and subtelomeric regions. However, the boundaries were almost always sharp; the last perfect telomeric repeat was juxtaposed to unrelated sequences in the subtelomeric region (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM5">S4</a>). This pattern would emerge if the entire telomeric region was occasionally forged by the telomerase de novo in the germline.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Centromeres</h3><p>There was one specific region greatly enriched for repeats and impoverished for genes in each <i>J. regia</i> (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">14</a>) and <i>J. microcarpa</i> pseudomolecule. These regions were at similar locations on the corresponding <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> pseudomolecules, contained the same sequences, and had similar structure (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">15</a>). They coincided with the locations of the <i>J. regia</i> centromeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Synteny analysis in Rosids with a walnut physical map reveals slow genome evolution in long-lived woody perennials. BMC Genom. 16, 707 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1672">17</a></sup>. To further characterize these regions, we generated self-synteny dot-plots for a 5-Mb region surrounding each repeat peak. These analyses revealed the existence of clusters of a 12.5-kb <i>Gypsy</i> LTR-RT (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig5">5a</a>), which we name <i>JCR</i> (Juglandoid Centromeric Repeat) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig5">5b</a>). <i>JCR</i>s were nearly exclusively located in the centromeres. For instance, we found 62 complete <i>JCRs</i> within a 3-Mb centromeric region of Jr2D (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig5">5a</a>), but only four copies in the remaining 41 Mb of that pseudomolecule. Analogous CRs of the <i>Ty/Gypsy</i> superfamily have been located in the centromere cores in grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Presting, G. G., Malysheva, L., Fuchs, J. &amp; Schubert, I. Z. A TY3/GYPSY retrotransposon-like sequence localizes to the centromeric regions of cereal chromosomes. Plant J. 16, 721–728 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1701">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Sharma, A. &amp; Presting, G. G. Evolution of centromeric retrotransposons in grasses. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 1335–1352 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1704">30</a></sup>, cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Luo, S. et al. The cotton centromere contains a Ty3-gypsy-like LTR retroelement. PLoS. ONE 7, 35261 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1709">31</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Tek, A. L., Kashihara, K., Murata, M. &amp; Nagaki, K. Functional centromeres in soybean include two distinct tandem repeats and a retrotransposon. Chrom. Res. 18, 337–347 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1713">32</a></sup>, beet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Weber, B. &amp; Schmidt, T. Nested Ty3-gypsy retrotransposons of a single Beta procumbens centromere contain a putative chromodomain. Chrom. Res. 17, 379–396 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1717">33</a></sup>, and <i>Brassica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of the centromere and peri-centromere retrotransposons in Brassica rapa and their distribution in related Brassica species. Plant J. 49, 173–183 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1723">34</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Centromere structure and evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Centromere structure and evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="534" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of the <i>JCR</i> repeats in the centromeric region of Jr2D. <b>b</b> Structure of the centromeric <i>JCR Gypsy</i> element. <b>c</b> and <b>d</b> Structural comparisons of 5-Mb intervals including the <i>JCR</i> arrays between <i>J. regia</i> homoeologous chromosome pairs Jr8D-Jr8S and Jr2D-Jr2S. Lines connect collinear loci in homoeologous chromosomes. The coordinates of the edges of the 5-Mb intervals in Mb are shown. <b>c</b> The Jr8D-Jr8S homoeologous chromosome pair illustrating the conservation of the centromere location on homoeologous chromosomes. A <i>JCR</i> array is located in the interval Jr8DG00090000-Jr8DG00105900 on Jr8D. This interval corresponds to interval Jr8SG00067200-Jr8SG00077500 on Jr8S, which also contains a <i>JCR</i> array. <b>d</b> The Jr2D-Jr2S homoeologous chromosome pair illustrating the repositioning of the <i>JCR</i> array into different interval without perturbation of the order of collinear genes. In Jr2D, the <i>JCR</i> array is within interval Jr2DG00174800-Jr2DG00176500. This interval corresponds to interval Jr2SG00125900-Jr2SG00126200 in Jr2S, which is devoid of a JCR array.
