Instead, a JCR array is in interval Jr2SG00133300-Jr2SG00138800 in pseudomolecule Jr2S</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We located 403 protein-coding genes, many conserved and showing collinearity with genes in the grape genome, within the <i>JCR</i> arrays (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM2">S1</a>). For 201 of them, we found transcripts in two or more of the 22 RNAseq datasets we analyzed, indicating that many of these genes are expressed (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM6">S5</a>).</p><p>We used genes located within and near the <i>JCR</i> arrays and collinear on Serr dominant and subdominant homoeologous pseudomolecules as landmarks for comparisons of the locations of homoeologous centromeres within the Serr pseudomolecules (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig5">5c, d</a>). In the homoeologous chromosome pairs Jr4D-Jr4S, Jr5D-Jr5S, and Jr8D-Jr8S (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig5">5c</a>), the <i>JCR</i> array was located in corresponding intervals (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">16</a>). In the remaining pairs, the <i>JCR</i> array was located in differing locations. In the Jr2D-Jr2S pair the array was repositioned to a new location without perturbing the order of the collinear genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig5">5d</a>). In that pair, centromere repositioning likely occurred by an intrachromosomal translocation of the <i>JCR</i> array into a new location. In homoeologous chromosome pairs Jr1D-Jr1S, Jr6D-Jr6S, and Jr7D-Jr7S, the <i>JCR</i> array was repositioned to a new location by an inversion with one breakpoint within the <i>JCR</i> array (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">16</a>). If a break were at the edge of the JCR array, such an inversion may reposition the entire <i>JCR</i> block and the entire centromere. If the break were in the middle of the array, it would create pair of <i>JCR</i> arrays, as seen in Jr1S (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">16</a>). In this specific case, it needs to be determined which of the <i>JCR</i> arrays is the active centromere and which is silent.</p><p>We frequently found inversions to terminate in the centromeric regions, suggesting that centromeric regions were prone to rearrangements (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM2">S1</a>). The centromeric regions were also prone to gene transposition as shown by the comparison of gene collinearity between the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 pseudomolecules (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">17</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Disease resistance genes</h3><p>Since <i>J. microcarpa</i> is important for the development of disease-resistant rootstocks for walnut commercial production, we paid special attention to resistance gene analogues (RGA, henceforth R genes) in the two genomes (File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM7">S6</a>). There was very little difference in the number of R genes between a cultivated and a wild species. There were 942 R genes in JrSerr_v1.0 and 903 in Jm31.01_v1.0. The R genes represented 3% of the total number of genes in both genomes.</p><p>We classified the R genes into 11 categories (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S22</a>). The most frequent category was the receptor-like kinase (RLK), which represented 62 and 58% of the R genes in JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies.</p><p>R genes were present on all chromosomes, but showed a tendency to be located in the distal regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig6">6</a>). We divided the total number of genes present in a chromosome arm into 20 windows containing equal numbers of genes and computed the proportion of R genes in each window. The proportions correlated with bin locations on the telomere-to-centromere axis in the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules (<i>r</i> = −0.48, <i>p</i> = 0.033) but in the Jm31.01_v1.0 pseudomolecules the correlation was not significant (<i>r</i> <i>=</i> −0.35, <i>p</i> = 0.095). The distribution of R gene was similar between homoeologous <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> pseudomolecules, but, there was no similarity in their distribution between homoeologous chromosomes within a genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#Fig6">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="The distribution of the R genes along J. regia Serr (a) and J. microcarpa 31.01 (b) chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: The distribution of the R genes along <i>J. regia</i> Serr (<b>a</b>) and <i>J. microcarpa</i> 31.01 (<b>b</b>) chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0139-1/MediaObjects/41438_2019_139_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="243" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Heat maps show the numbers of R genes per 1 Mb non-overlapping windows along the pseudomolecules. The starting nucleotides of the pseudomolecules are to the left. Centromeres are indicated by black ovals</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Of the 942 R genes in the JrSerr_v1.0 assembly, 793 (84.2%) were in collinear location in the Jm31.01_v1.0 assembly. This was nearly identical to the 84.4% collinearity for the remaining 30,345 genes in the Serr genome. A total of 378 (40.1%) of the R genes were in collinear locations on the Serr homoeologous chromosomes. Of the remaining Serr genes, 39.0% were in collinear locations on the homoeologous chromosomes. Therefore, there appears to be no difference in dynamism of the R gene families and the rest of the <i>Juglans</i> genes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Sequencing approach</h3><p>Sequencing of heterozygous <i>Juglans</i> genomes using the short-read technology resulted in assemblies with short contigs and scaffolds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1966">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1969">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Bai, W. N. et al. Demographically idiosyncratic responses to climate change and rapid Pleistocene diversification of the walnut genus Juglans (Juglandaceae) revealed by whole-genome sequences. New Phytol. 217, 1726–1736 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1972">12</a></sup>. Similar results were previously obtained for other outcrossing woody perennials with the Sanger sequencing technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1976">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1979">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Velasco, E. A. A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS. ONE 2, 326 (2007)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1979_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1982">37</a></sup>, which is often considered as the gold standard of DNA sequencing. We attribute the great increase in the contiguity of our assemblies compared to the previous attempts to sequence <i>Juglans</i> genomes to the use of a long-read sequencing technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Minio, A., Lin, J., Gaut, B. S. &amp; Cantu, D. How Single molecule real-time sequencing and haplotype phasing have enabled reference-grade diploid genome assembly of wine grapes. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 826 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d27278e1989">38</a></sup> in addition to the haploid state of the <i>J. regia</i> and <i>J. microcarpa</i> genomes in the interspecific hybrid.</p><p>A well-known problem accompanying the long-read sequencing technologies is high error rates. These errors must be corrected during genome assembly. We were able to correct most of the errors during polishing of the assemblies and achieved a rate of one error in 10,000 nucleotides, which matched a standard recommended for the human genome for the Sanger sequencing technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Quality assessment of the human genome sequency. Nature 429, 365–368 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2003">39</a></sup>. In both genomes, 98% of errors that remained in the assembly after polishing were indels, indicating that the polishing algorithm was more efficient  in detecting base substitution errors than base indel errors. We corrected both types of errors by mapping Illumina reads into the polished assemblies.</p><p>Long contigs obtained with the PacBio long-read technology for the <i>J. microcarpa</i> × <i>J. regia</i> hybrid were critical for efficient aligning sequence contigs on the optical maps of the parental species and their allocation to parental genomes. We were able to allocate 99.85% of the hybrid genome sequence to the parental genomes. Contigs that are too short to be aligned on optical maps can be allocated to parental genomes by aligning them on Illumina reads of the parental genomes. In our case, we used the published Illumina sequence of <i>J. regia</i> cv Chandler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2019">11</a></sup> for allocating short contigs to parental genomes.</p><p>The optical maps played additional roles in our genome sequence assembly. They facilitated construction of long scaffolds, which reached N50 = 34.8 Mb and approached or equaled the lengths of the chromosomes. They also facilitated detection of misassembled contigs and the estimation of the actual lengths of most of the gaps. By using two independently made optical maps for each genome (one for the hybrid and one for each parent) we were able to decide whether a discrepancy between an optical map and a sequence was due to an error in the sequence or in the map.</p><p>A synergy between long-read sequencing technology and optical mapping made it possible to leverage the haploid state of the genomes in the <i>J. microcarpa</i> × <i>J. regia</i> hybrid to produce remarkably complete genome assemblies. A concern about using a hybrid for the construction of a reference genome sequence is that in some instances one of the parental genomes is partially or completely eliminated from a hybrid. The <i>Hordeum vulgare</i> × <i>H. bulbosum</i> hybrids are the best known example<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ho, K. M. &amp; Kasha, K. J. Genetic control of chromosome elimination during haploid formation in barley. Genetics 81, 263–275 (1975)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2042">40</a></sup>. Loss of 10% or more of DNA from a genome in artificial polyploids produced by the colchicine treatment of interspecific hybrids has been reported and may be another reason for concern<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song, K. M., Lu, P., Tang, K. L. &amp; Osborn, T. C. Rapid genome change in synthetic polyploids of Brassica and its implications for polyploid evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 92, 7719–7723 (1995)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2047">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, B. et al. Rapid genomic changes in newly synthesized amphiploids of Triticum and Aegilops. I. changes in low-copy noncoding DNA sequences. Genome 41, 272–277 (1998)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2047_1">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shaked, H., Kashkush, K., Ozkan, H., Feldman, M. &amp; Levy, A. A. Sequence elimination and cytosine methylation are rapid and reproducible responses of the genome to wide hybridization and allopolyploidy. Plant Cell 13, 1750–1759 (2001)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2047_2">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Osborn, T. C. et al. Understanding mechanisms of novel gene expression in polyploids. Trends Genet. 19, 141–147 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2050">44</a></sup>. To ascertain whether the genome of our hybrid was complete, we compared the total length of its optical map to the sum of the lengths of the optical maps of the parents. The maps differed by a mere 0.09%, which is below the error rate of these measurements. We therefore concluded that the hybrid genome was a complete representation of the parental genomes. The main source of genetic instability in hybrids and polyploids is meiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Oleszczuk, S. &amp; Lukaszewski, A. J. The origin of unusual chromosome constitutions among newly formed allopolyploids. Am. J. Bot. 101, 318–326 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2054">45</a></sup>. Since DNA used for the construction of sequencing libraries and optical maps is isolated from young somatic tissues this cause of instability is irrelevant. Nevertheless, the possibility of somatic instability of an interspecific hybrid should be kept in mind and the additivity of the hybrid genomes should be checked. Making the use of the optical map trio, as we did, is the most practical approach, because it requires very little additional work.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Juglans genome evolution</h3><p>We showed that <i>Juglans</i> genomes consist of eight pairs of homoeologous chromosomes, which originated by the Juglandoid WGD. The chromosomes were subjected to a highly asymmetric fractionation. Similar asymmetric fractionation following WGD was described in other species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Langham, R. J. et al. Genomic duplication, fractionation and the origin of regulatory novelty. Genetics 166, 935–945 (2004)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2069">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dvorak, J., Yang, Z.-L., You, F. M. &amp; Luo, M. C. Deletion polymorphism in wheat chromosome regions with contrasting recombination rates. Genetics 168, 1665–1675 (2004)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2069_1">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cheng, F. et al. Biased gene fractionation and dominant gene expression among the subgenomes of Brassica rapa. PLoS. ONE 7, 36442 (2012)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2069_2">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Renny-Byfield, S., Gong, L., Gallagher, J. P. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Persistence of subgenomes in paleopolyploid cotton after 60 My of evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 1063–1071 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2072">49</a></sup>. In wheat, the larger B genome has lost twice as much DNA as the smaller A genome since the origin of tetraploid wheat about 0.3 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Dvorak, J., Yang, Z.-L., You, F. M. &amp; Luo, M. C. Deletion polymorphism in wheat chromosome regions with contrasting recombination rates. Genetics 168, 1665–1675 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2076">47</a></sup>. If a difference in subgenome sizes were the cause of asymmetric gene loss in walnut, the subgenomes should differ in gene density, since large genomes are less gene dense than small genomes. That was not the case. Gene density was similar in the dominant and subdominant homoeologues.</p><p>We showed that the asymmetric fractionation of <i>Juglans</i> subgenomes has not relented in the recent past. The factor that caused the asymmetry is therefore likely active in the current <i>Juglans</i> genomes. We suggest that the factor is asymmetric gene expression on dominant and subdominant chromosomes. An asymmetry in expression of genes on dominant and subdominant homoeologues has been reported in plant families as diverse as Poaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2089">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Renny-Byfield, S., Rodgers-Melnick, E. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Gene fractionation and function in the ancient subgenomes of maize. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1825–1832 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2092">51</a></sup>, Brassicaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Cheng, F. et al. Biased gene fractionation and dominant gene expression among the subgenomes of Brassica rapa. PLoS. ONE 7, 36442 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2096">48</a></sup>, and Malvaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Renny-Byfield, S., Gong, L., Gallagher, J. P. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Persistence of subgenomes in paleopolyploid cotton after 60 My of evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 1063–1071 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2100">49</a></sup>. A relevant question is what causes the asymmetry in gene expression. In <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Brassica</i>, reduced gene expression was linked to the silencing effects of small, 24-nt RNAs amplified against specific LTR-RTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Woodhouse, M. R. et al. Origin, inheritance, and gene regulatory consequences of genome dominance in polyploids (vol 111, pg 5283, 2014). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 6527–6527 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2111">52</a></sup>. The methylation of LTR-RTs, and their contribution to the pool of siRNAs in walnut should be analyzed to determine if the same mechanism is the basis for the differences in gene expression in the <i>Juglans</i> subgenomes.</p><p>The rates of accumulation of structural changes in the <i>Juglans</i> genomes ranged from 0.4 to 1.4 major rearrangements per MY during the 66 MY of evolution. These rate estimates are among the lowest we have obtained to date<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498–502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2124">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Dvorak, J. et al. Structural variation and rates of genome evolution in the grass family seen through comparison of sequences of genomes greatly differing in size. Plant J. 95, 487–503 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2127">15</a></sup>. Furthermore, 43.7% of the <i>J. regia</i> genes were collinear with genes on the grape pseudomolecules, which is remarkable considering that the two lineages diverged more than 100 MYA. This high gene collinearity among woody perennials contrasts with low gene collinearity in comparisons involving genomes of herbaceous plants, such as those of grasses (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S16</a>), and provide additional evidence for slow genome evolution in woody perennials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Synteny analysis in Rosids with a walnut physical map reveals slow genome evolution in long-lived woody perennials. BMC Genom. 16, 707 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2137">17</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Trees</h3><p>DJUG 31.01 is a tree grown from <i>J. microcarpa</i> seeds collected near Sanderson TX USA by Loy Shreve as accession 83–131. Serr is the result of a cross between <i>J. regia</i> cv Payne of unknown seedling origin and PI 159568, a USDA plant introduction of <i>J. regia</i> from Paghman, Afghanistan. We generated clones of their hybrid MS1-56 by micropropagating the epicotyl excised from an immature hybrid embryo and transplanted rooted clones into pots in a greenhouse.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Optical map construction</h3><p>We collected leaves from tissue culture-grown clones for MS1-56 and orchard trees for Serr and 31.01 and provided them to Amplicon Express (Pullman WA USA) for high-molecular-weight (HMW) (&gt;500 kb) DNA isolation. Based on the frequency of recognition sites in the genome sequence of <i>J. regia</i> cv Chandler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2174">11</a></sup>, we selected nicking endonucleases Nt.<i>Bsp</i>QI and Nb.<i>Bss</i>SI (New England BioLabs, Ipswich MA USA) for DNA nicking. We treated HMW DNA of MS1-56 separately with both enzymes and that of Serr and 31.01 only with Nt.<i>Bsp</i>QI. We stained nicked molecules according to the IrysPrep Reagent Kit instructions [Bionano Genomics, San Diego CA USA, (henceforth BNG)] and imaged the stained DNA molecules with the Irys system (BNG). To obtain basic labelling and length information, we converted molecules &gt; 20 kb into bnx files with AutoDetect (BNG) software. We then aligned molecules &gt; 180 kb that passed the quality control against each other, clustered them, and assembled them into contigs using the BNG assembly pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771–776 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2188">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Cao, H. Z. et al. Rapid detection of structural variation in a human genome using nanochannel-based genome mapping technology. Gigascience 3, 34 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2191">54</a></sup>. We used the <i>p-</i>values 1 × 10<sup>–8</sup>, 1 × 10<sup>–9</sup>, and 1 × 10<sup>–15</sup> as thresholds for pairwise assembly, extension/refinement, and final contig refinement, respectively. We manually checked the resulting optical maps for chimeric contigs and resolved them when detected.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Optical map length comparisons</h3><p>Optical maps of Serr and 31.01 consisted of diploid and haploid regions. We self-aligned optical maps to identify diploid regions, decided which of the two haplotypes in the phased regions were present on the MS1-56 optical map, and disregarded the alternative haplotype. The sum of the edited Serr and 31.01 optical map lengths was compared with the length of the MS1-56 optical map.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Pacific biosciences and 10X genomics sequencing</h3><p>We isolated nuclear DNAs from MS1-56 as described earlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Dvorak, J., McGuire, P. E. &amp; Cassidy, B. Apparent sources of the A genomes of wheats inferred from the polymorphism in abundance and restriction fragment length of repeated nucleotide sequences. Genome 30, 680–689 (1988)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2220">55</a></sup> and prepared sequencing libraries using protocols from Pacific Biosciences (Menlo Park CA USA, henceforth). They were sequenced with the Single Molecule Real-Time (SMRT) technology at the Genome Center, University of California, Davis on the PacBio long-read sequencing instrument RSII using the P6 Polymerase Binding and C4 Chemistry Kits. Sequencing of the 58 PacBio SMRT cells of MS1-56 generated 57.2 Gb (~54X coverage) of reads averaging 12,791 bp with a maximum length of 82,702 bp. We assembled 2.05 million error-corrected qualified reads with Falcon (v0.4.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2224">56</a></sup> into 460 contigs (total length of 1,056,053,412 bp), which we polished with the genomic consensus tool Quiver (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>). We named this assembly MS1-56_v0.</p><p>To prepare 10X Genomics sequencing library, we isolated DNA from leaves of orchard-grown MS1-56. We prepared a sequencing library according to a protocol from 10X Genomics (Pleasanton CA USA). We sequenced the library with Illumina HiSeq4000 and assembled 680.53 million reads (89X genome equivalent, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S1</a>) with the 10X Genomics software Supernova (v1.1.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Weisenfeld, N. I., Kumar, V., Shah, P., Church, D. M. &amp; Jaffe, D. B. Direct determination of diploid genome sequences. Genome Res. 27, 757–767 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2241">57</a></sup> using default parameters. The assembly produced 79,926 contigs with a total length of 1,005,111,912 bp and an N50 of 41,627 bp.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Scaffolding</h3><p>We digested 460 MS1-56 sequence contigs in assembly MS1-56_v0 in silico with the Nt.<i>Bsp</i>QI and Nb.<i>Bss</i>SI restriction endonucleases using Knickers (BNG) and aligned the contigs on the MS-156 Nt.<i>Bsp</i>QI and Nb.<i>Bss</i>SI optical maps using RefAligner (BNG). We visually checked the alignments in IrysView (BNG). Of the 460 MS1-56 polished contigs, 239 (~1047 Mb) were of sufficient length to be aligned. Five of the aligned contigs were chimeric and we disjoined them, increasing the total number of polished PacBio contigs to 465 (assembly MS1-56_v1, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S2</a>). The contig N50 = 7,904,778 bp. We then aligned MS1-56_v1 contigs on optical maps and scaffolded them with the hybrid-scaffolding pipeline (BNG). Since two optical maps were generated for MS1-56, we performed a two-step hybrid-scaffolding resolution with the strictest ‘conflict-free’ setting, first for sequence contigs aligned on the Nt.<i>Bsp</i>QI optical map. We then aligned scaffolds on the Nb.<i>Bss</i>SI optical map and scaffolded them further. We performed scaffolding also in the reverse order, using the Nb.<i>Bss</i>SI optical map for aligning sequence contigs, which was then followed by scaffolding on the Nt.<i>Bsp</i>QI optical map. We found no difference between the two approaches. BNG (<a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/">https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/</a>) provided us with software packages for scaffolding.</p><p>To stitch the scaffolds, we trained the filtering parameters of Stitch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Shelton, J. M. et al. Tools and pipelines for BioNano data: molecule assembly pipeline and FASTA super scaffolding tool. BMC Genom. 16, 734 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2292">58</a></sup> to be suitable for the MS1-56 genome and then performed Stitch in iterations until we could produce no additional scaffolds. After each round of Stitch, we manually checked the ‘potentially chimeric scaffolds’ flagged by the program and resolved them if necessary. This sequential use of Stitch reduced the number of scaffolds from 465 to 264. The total length of this assembly (MS1-56_scf) was 1,066,408,726 bp, N50 = 34,776,948 bp, and 0.97% Ns.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Phasing MS1-56 scaffolds into parental genomes</h3><p>We aligned the 264 scaffolds of the MS1-56_scf assembly on the Serr and 31.01 optical maps using RefAligner (BNG). Of the 1,066,408,726 bp of the MS1-56 sequence, we allocated 536,956,484,537 bp present in 19 scaffolds to Serr and 527,846,219 bp, present in 21 scaffolds, to 31.01. This accounted for 99.85% of the assembly. The remaining 224 MS1-56 scaffolds had a total length of 1,606,023 bp (0.15% of the assembly) and were too short to be aligned on optical maps. We allocated those that could be aligned on the sequence of Chandler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2304">11</a></sup> (88 scaffolds) and the remainder (136) we allocated to 31.01 by default. Thus, we allocated all 264 scaffolds to one of the two parental genomes: 107 to the Serr genome (JrSerr_v0.1), totaling 538,152,081 bp, and 157 to the 31.01 genome (Jm31.01_v0.1) totaling 528,256,645 bp.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Pseudomolecule construction, gap closing, and error corrections</h3><p>Eighteen Serr scaffolds equivalent to 532,266,604 bp and representing 98.9% of the Serr assembly and 19 of the 31.01 scaffolds equivalent to 523,690,948 bp and representing 99.1% of the 31.01 assembly shared SNP markers with the <i>J. regia</i> genetic and physical maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Synteny analysis in Rosids with a walnut physical map reveals slow genome evolution in long-lived woody perennials. BMC Genom. 16, 707 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2319">17</a></sup> and a <i>J. microcarpa</i> genotyping-by-sequencing-produced map. We used these SNPs to ordered and orient scaffolds on the maps to construct pseudomolecules. The remaining 89 scaffolds (5,885,477 bp) of Serr and 138 scaffolds (4,565,697 bp) of 31.01 were short and could not be included into the pseudomolecules. There were two gaps of unknown size in the JrSerr_v0.1 pseudomolecules and three such gaps in the Jm31.01_v0.1 pseudomolecules, which we filled with 1,000 Ns. We estimated the lengths of the remaining 102 gaps in the JrSerr_v0.1 pseudomolecules and 106 in the Jm31.01_v0.1 pseudomolecules based on sequence alignments on the optical maps. The numbers and lengths of these gaps were reduced with unassigned scaffolds or sequence contigs assembled from 10X Genomics reads to 54 in both pseudomolecule sets with software FGAP v1.8.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Piro, V. C. et al. FGAP: an automated gap closing tool. BMC Res. Notes 7, 371 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2326">59</a></sup>.</p><p>To correct errors remaining in the pseudomolecule sequences, we mapped about 93.9 Gb of Illumina reads (89 × ) onto the JrSerr_v0.1 and Jm31.01_v0.1 pseudomolecules using BWA v0.7.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2333">60</a></sup>. We removed the duplicate reads present among the Illumina reads generated by PCR with SAMtools 0.1.19<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2337">61</a></sup>. We performed single sample variant calling with SAMtools and BCFtools 0.1.19<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. A statistical framework for SNP calling, mutation discovery, association mapping and population genetical parameter estimation from sequencing data. Bioinformatics 27, 2987–2993 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2341">62</a></sup> and kept variants with read depth &gt; 10 and &lt;200 and genotype quality &gt; 30 and discarded the rest. We also discarded heterozygous read stacks. We aligned Illumina reads on the pseudomolecule sequences and corrected base substitutions and indels in the pseudomolecules with VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2345">63</a></sup>, which generated the final version of pseudomolecules and unanchored scaffolds JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">TE annotation and SSR discovery</h3><p>We identified repeated sequences in the JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies by a combination of homolog-based and <i>de novo</i> approaches. We used the software RepeatModeler v1.0.10 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler</a>) to obtain TE consensus sequences. We deployed MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Han, Y. J. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, 199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2367">64</a></sup> to identify Miniature Inverted Repeat Transposable Elements (MITEs) and SINE-Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wenke, T. et al. Targeted identification of short interspersed nuclear element families shows their widespread existence and extreme heterogeneity in plant genomes. Plant Cell 23, 3117–3128 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2371">65</a></sup> to identify Short Interspersed Nuclear Elements (SINEs). We manually inspected the outputs to eliminate artifacts and combined the TEs which passed the quality control with the current release of Repbase v22.06<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2375">66</a></sup> and the <i>J. regia</i> repetitive library to produce a custom TE library. We used the custom library to predict TEs with software RepeatMasker v4.0.7 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). We classified the TE sequences according to the system proposed by Wicker et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2390">67</a></sup>.</p><p>We used the MISA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Thiel, T., Michalek, W., Varshney, R. K. &amp; Graner, A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 106, 411–422 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2397">68</a></sup> software to identify microsatellite motifs in the assemblies with the following minimum repeat numbers: ten for monomers, six for the di-, and five for the tri-, tetra-, penta-, and hexanucleotide motifs. We used a 1-Mb sliding window to calculate the SSR density on each chromosome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Gene annotation and transcription factor and R gene identification</h3><p>We used both homology-based and <i>de novo</i> methods to predict protein-coding genes in the TE masked JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 assemblies. We extracted homologous protein sequences for eudicots from NCBI and used them as training sets in Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2412">69</a></sup>. We used a <i>J. regia</i> gene set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2419">11</a></sup> to create species-specific parameters for Augustus and used these parameters also in <i>de novo</i> gene prediction by Maker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: An easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2427">70</a></sup>. For homology-based gene annotation, we used the transcripts of <i>J. regia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2433">11</a></sup> to generate predicted homology-based gene models in Maker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: An easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2437">70</a></sup>. Finally, we merged the gene models from de novo prediction and homology-based prediction to produce a comprehensive and non-redundant gene set by using Maker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: An easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2441">70</a></sup>.</p><p>We used the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) v3 tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Simão, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2448">71</a></sup> to identify universal single copy orthologs (USCOs) in the assembly as a measure of completeness. BUSCO analysis was performed using the plant dataset (embryophyta_odb9), which excludes the <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> and <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> and contained 1440 BUSCO groups.</p><p>We used DNA-binding domains in Pfam 31.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database: towards a more sustainable future. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D279–D285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2461">72</a></sup> to identify transcription factors (TFs) among the protein-coding genes annotated in JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0 using BLAST. We classified the TFs according to the Plant Transcription Factor Databases (plantTFDB, <a href="http://planttfdb.cbi.edu.cn/">http://planttfdb.cbi.edu.cn/</a>).</p><p>R genes were identified using the prediction pipeline RGAugury<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Li, P. C. et al. RGAugury: a pipeline for genome-wide prediction of resistance gene analogs (RGAs) in plants. BMC Genom. 17, 852 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2475">73</a></sup>. Amino acid sequences of the protein coding genes in the whole genomes and their coordinates on the genomes (gff3 files) were used as the input. An E value of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup> was used for the protein BLAST search against four protein databases: pfam, smart, gene3d, and superfamily. Four types of RGAs were predicted, including nucleotide‐binding sites encoding genes, RLK, receptor‐like protein, and transmembrane coiled‐coil protein through screening for the presence of major domains, such as NB‐ARC, toll‐interleukin receptor, leucine‐rich repeats, coiled‐coil domain, transmembrane domain, serine/threonine kinase domain, and LysM motif.</p><p>To determine if the distribution of the R genes along chromosomes mirrored that of other genes, the total number of genes along a chromosome arm were divided into 20 windows, each containing the same number of genes. The proportion of R genes in each window was computed and averaged across the 32 chromosome arms. Rank correlation coefficients (<i>r</i>) for the window location on the telomere-to-centromere axis and the mean proportion of R genes in the window were computed for both genome assemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Dot-plots and circular plots</h3><p>To construct synteny dot-plots, we downloaded protein sequences for <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Genoscope)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2499">18</a></sup> and <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> v1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Albert, V. A. et al. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1467 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2506">19</a></sup> from Phytozome v12.0 and used them in BLASTP searches as targets while genes from JrSerr_v1.0 were used as queries. We also aligned JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0, in which we used the former as a query source and the latter as a target in BLASTP with an E value of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>. We analyzed all alignments with MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2513">74</a></sup> with default parameters. We also use MCScanX with default parameters to find collinear blocks containing at least five collinear gene pairs. We visualized syntenic blocks in Circos 0.69<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: An information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2517">75</a></sup> and constructed the dot-plots with dot_plotter in MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2521">74</a></sup>.</p><p>To identify WGD within <i>J. regia</i>, we searched homology by BLASTP using HC genes annotated on the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules both as queries and targets (self-search). We performed the same analysis with HC genes annotated on the Jm31.01_v1.0 pseudomolecules. We used a filter threshold of 1 × 10<sup>–5</sup> to identify homologous proteins. The longest protein sequences were analyzed with MCScanX with default parameters. We considered at least five genes in an ascending or descending order as a syntenic block. We constructed the syntenic block maps with Circos 0.69 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: An information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2533">75</a></sup> and dot-plots with dot_plotter in MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2537">74</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Molecular clock rate</h3><p>We used the JrSerr_v1.0, Jm31.01_v1.0, <i>V. vinifera</i>, and <i>A. trichopoda</i> gene sequences to calculate the synonymous (<i>Ks</i>) substitution rates. We identified orthologous genes among these four species using Proteinortho5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Lechner, M. et al. Proteinortho: detection of (co-)orthologs in large-scale analysis. BMC Bioinforma. 12, 124 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2559">76</a></sup>. We identified orthologous inter-genome gene pairs and paralogous intra-genome gene pairs by bi-directional BLAST with the default parameter. We aligned amino-acid sequences of each pair of genes using ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Thompson, J. D., Higgins, D. G. &amp; Gibson, T. J. Improved sensitivity of profile searches through the use of sequence weights and gap excision. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 10, 19–29 (1994)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2563">77</a></sup> and transformed amino-acid alignments into nucleotide sequence alignments according to coding sequences (CDS). We then calculated the <i>Ka</i> and <i>Ks</i> of each pair of genes using Kimura 2 parameter model in KaKs_Calculator2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Wang, D., Zhang, Y., Zhang, Z., Zhu, J. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genom., Proteom., Bioinforma. 8, 77–80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2574">78</a></sup>. We computed genome divergence times and dated the Juglandoid WGD or γWGT according to <i>r</i> = <i>Ks</i>/2<i>t</i>, where <i>r</i> is the rate of nucleotide substitution per synonymous site per year, <i>Ks</i> is the number of substitutions per synonymous site, and <i>t</i> is time of species divergence in years. We used the same approach to compute <i>r</i> from <i>Ka</i>. Using the time estimate and the average <i>Ka</i> and <i>Ks</i> between paralogues on the homoeologous chromosomes in the Serr and 31.01 genomes, we computed clock rates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Gene collinearity and structural chromosome analyses</h3><p>The methodology used for gene collinearity analysis has been described earlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498–502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2618">14</a></sup>, and we will provide only essential information. We used the amino-acid sequences of 31,286 HC genes annotated in the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules as a query and the amino acid sequences of grape HC genes as targets in BLASTP homology searches. We used a default BLASTP parameter setting. We also performed BLSTP search using JrSerr_v1.0 as a query against itself (target). We recorded the top three alignments. In the BLASTP searches using JrSerr_v1.0 as a query against Jm31.01_v1.0 as a target, we recorded only the top hit.</p><p>We analyzed collinearity as follows. We considered three or more genes to be collinear if the starting nucleotides of the top hits followed an ascending or descending order and distances between them were &lt;0.5 Mb, allowing non-collinear genes to interrupt a sequence of collinear genes. If a gene was homologous to tandem duplicated genes on a target pseudomolecule, we recorded only one of the duplicated genes as collinear, provided that it was in a collinear position on the pseudomolecule.</p><p>We constructed a spreadsheet of datasets showing collinearity of each of the 31,286 genes on the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules with those on the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules (self), with those on the Jm31.01_v1.0 pseudomolecules, and the grape pseudomolecules. We colored spreadsheet cells containing collinear genes whereas those that were not collinear we left colorless. We indicated changes in gene order due to inversions or translocations by changes in cell color. We recorded for each rearrangement the register of the starting and ending gene and rearrangement type. We coded the rearrangements as follows. A = inversion of 2 genes, B = inversion of 3 genes, C = inversion of &gt; 3 genes, D = translocation of 2 genes within a chromosome, E = translocation of 3 genes within a chromosome, F = translocation &gt; 3 genes within a chromosome, iT = interstitial translocation between chromosomes, T = terminal translocation, Dup = duplication of a segment, and Del = deletion of a segment. To quantify collinearity, we counted genes of JrSerr_v1.0 collinear with a specific genome and expressed them as a percentage of all HC genes present on the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules.</p><p>We compared the order of collinear genes along pseudomolecules within JrSerr_v1.0, between JrSerr_v1.0 and Jm31.01_v1.0, and between JrSerr_v1.0 and the grape pseudomolecules and determined the most parsimonious origin of a rearrangement, provided that information on the rearrangement was available in all genomes and subgenomes. We coded the branch assignments as follows: <i>J. microcarpa</i> (M), <i>J. regia</i> (R), and the branch preceding the divergence of the two (RM). We used only major rearrangements (C, F, and iT) in computing rates. We computed the rearrangement rate acquisition by a branch using time estimates in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#MOESM1">S13</a>.</p><p>We used the following approach to quantify gene collinearity between the JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecules and the grape pseudomolecules. We recorded only the top hits in an effort to identify the orthologous pseudomolecule among the three homoeologous pseudomolecules in the grape genome. Since this approach still produced multiple alignments for every JrSerr_v1.0 pseudomolecule in the grape genome, we selected the alignment that contained the largest number of collinear genes and eliminated the remaining alignments present. We color coded the collinear genes on the orthologous alignments and counted them.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec27">Fractionation of the Juglandoid WGD</h3><p>To investigate the distribution of the differences in gene numbers along the dominant and subdominant chromosomes, we subdivided each homoeologous chromosome pair into <i>N</i> intervals between <i>N</i> + 1 collinear genes shared by the homoeologous chromosome pairs in the Serr genome. We then counted the number of unique genes (henceforth singletons) present in an interval and plotted the numbers for each homoeologue. We tested the significance of the difference in singleton numbers between the dominant and subdominant chromosomes with the paired <i>t</i>-test using the numbers in individual intervals in the dominant and subdominant chromosomes as variables.</p><p>To express the number of singletons per Mb rather than per interval between collinear genes, we subdivided the dominant chromosome into 2-Mb intervals and counted and plotted the number of singleton genes in the 2-Mb interval.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec28">Gene expression between dominant and subdominant chromosomes of <i>J. regia</i>
                        </h3><p>We downloaded the expression data for 22 RNAseq libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martinez-Garcia, P. J. et al. The walnut (Juglans regia) genome sequence reveals diversity in genes coding for the biosynthesis of non-structural polyphenols. Plant J. 87, 507–532 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2676">11</a></sup> and measured dominance in gene expression between pairs of collinear orthologues located on the eight homoeologous chromosome pairs of <i>J. regia</i>. We quantified gene expression level using FPKM values in Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–U174 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2683">79</a></sup>. We considered collinear orthologous pairs with greater than two-fold difference<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Zhang, T. Z. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531–U252 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2687">80</a></sup> as differentially expressed. We termed the gene that showed higher expression in differentially expressed gene pairs as the dominant orthologue whereas the gene with lower expression as the subordinate collinear orthologue. We counted the number of dominant genes in each chromosome and performed paired <i>t</i>-test to compare the difference in the number of dominant genes between dominant and subdominant subgenomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec29">Phylogeny reconstruction</h3><p>We downloaded the following genome assemblies for phylogenetic analysis and the construction of divergence chronology: <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2704">18</a></sup>, <i>Prunus persica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="International Peach Genome, I. et al. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 487–494 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2710">81</a></sup>, <i>Amborella trichopoda</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Albert, V. A. et al. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1467 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2716">19</a></sup>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2723">35</a></sup>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2729">82</a></sup>, <i>Malus domestica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099–1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2735">83</a></sup>, and <i>J. regia</i> Chandler, <i>J. sigillata</i>, <i>J. nigra</i>, <i>J. microcarpa</i> acc. 29.01, <i>J. hindsii</i>, <i>J. cathayensis</i>, <i>Pterocarya stenoptera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2760">5</a></sup>. With Proteinortho<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Lechner, M. et al. Proteinortho: detection of (co-)orthologs in large-scale analysis. BMC Bioinforma. 12, 124 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2765">76</a></sup> using default parameters we identified 1155 single-copy, orthologous genes shared by the <i>J. regia</i> (JrSerr_v1.0), <i>J. microcarpa</i> (Jm3101_v1.0), <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Malus domestica</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> genomes. We aligned amino-acid sequences of the 1,155 genes in the eight genomes with MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: A novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2794">84</a></sup>, removed regions with gaps, and concatenated the remaining sequences. We used the phylogenetic software IQ-TREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Nguyen, L. T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: A fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2798">85</a></sup> employing the Maximum-likelihood method to infer the phylogeny of the eight genomes and performed 1000 bootstrap replications to produce a consensus tree. We constructed the final phylogenetic tree with Figtree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Rambaut, A. FigTree v1.4.3. &#xA;                    http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2802">86</a></sup>.</p><p>Because neither pseudomolecules nor gene annotation was reported for <i>J. regia</i> Chandler, <i>J. sigillata</i>, <i>J. nigra</i>, <i>J. microcarpa</i> acc. 29.01, <i>J. hindsii</i>, <i>J. cathayensis</i>, and <i>Pterocarya stenoptera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Genomic variation among and within six Juglans species. G3: Genes|Genomes|Genetics (2018). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.118.200030&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2830">5</a></sup>, we aligned the CDS sequences of the 1,155 Serr and 31.01 genes to these seven genomic sequences to obtain corresponding CDS sequences. We then constructed a phylogenetic tree for the fifteen genomes as described above with the following changes. We converted the CDS sequences to amino-acid sequences. After removing partial alignments, all 15 genomes shared 809 single-copy, orthologous genes. We aligned the gene sequences with MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: A novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2834">84</a></sup>, removed regions with gaps, concatenated the remaining sequences, and constructed a tree.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec30">Divergence time estimation</h3><p>Based on fossil record, the diversification of Juglandaceae was estimated to have been initiated 80 MYA (71–96 MYA 95% CI)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Xiang, X. G. et al. Large-scale phylogenetic analyses reveal fagalean diversification promoted by the interplay of diaspores and environments in the Paleogene. Perspect. Plant Ecol. 16, 101–110 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2846">24</a></sup>. Since this estimate includes speciation of diploid <i>Rhoiptelea</i>, the WGD must have occurred after this date. Based on the chronogram in<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Xiang, X. G. et al. Large-scale phylogenetic analyses reveal fagalean diversification promoted by the interplay of diaspores and environments in the Paleogene. Perspect. Plant Ecol. 16, 101–110 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2853">24</a></sup> and fossil dating of the Engelhardioideae and Juglandoideae divergence to upper Cretaceous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Manchester, S. R. The fossil history of Juglandaceae. Monographs In Systematic Botany from the Missouri Botanical Garden. 21, 1–137 (1987)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2857">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Manos, P. S. et al. Phylogeny of extant and fossil Juglandaceae inferred from the integration of molecular and morphological data sets. Syst. Biol. 56, 412–430 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2860">87</a></sup> we arbitrarily chose 66 MYA for the origin of the Juglandoid WGD (reviewed in<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Chen, F. et al. Genomics: cracking the mysteries of walnuts. J. Genet. 98, (2019) in press." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2864">88</a></sup>). Based on the non-synonymous (<i>Ka</i>) and synonymous (<i>Ks</i>) divergence values computed for 1,429 collinear genes on the Serr homoeologous chromosome with KaKs Calculator 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Wang, D., Zhang, Y., Zhang, Z., Zhu, J. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genom., Proteom., Bioinforma. 8, 77–80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2875">78</a></sup>, we calibrated the molecular clock. We used MACSE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Ranwez, V., Harispe, S., Delsuc, F. &amp; Douzery, E. J. MACSE: multiple alignment of coding sequences accounting for frameshifts and stop codons. PLoS. ONE 6, 22594 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0139-1#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d27278e2879">89</a></sup> to perform multiple sequence alignment for each gene using the method of model averaging. The divergence (<i>k</i>) of pairs of orthologous genes was converted into time of divergence (<i>t</i>) in million years using formula: <i>t</i> = <i>k</i>/(2<i>r</i>)/10<sup>6</sup>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome assemblies and gene annotations of <i>J. regia</i> Serr and <i>J. microcarpa</i> acc. 31.01, and all sequencing data have been deposited under NCBI BioProject PRJNA413991. The assemblies, TE and gene annotations of <i>J. regia</i> Serr and <i>J. microcarpa</i> acc. 31.01 are also accessible via the JBroswer at <a href="http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/jbrowse/index.html?data=JrSerr&amp;loc">http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/jbrowse/index.html?data = JrSerr&amp;loc</a> and <a href="http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/jbrowse/index.html?data=Jm31.01&amp;loc">http://aegilops.wheat.ucdavis.edu/jbrowse/index.html?data = Jm31.01&amp;loc</a>.</p>

T.Z., L.W., C.A.L., L.C., K.R.D., and M.C.L. planned, organized, and conducted the sequencing steps. J.L. performed sequence assembly. T.Z., J.C.R., K.R.D., and M.-C.L. planned, organized, and conducted construction of Bionano optical maps. T.Z., L.W., and M.-C.L. conducted genome assembly. L.W. performed TE annotation. F.M.Y., L.W., S.C., B.B., C.Z.J., and A.M.D. contributed to gene annotations. L.W., F.M.Y., J.D., M.-C.L., P.J.B., and P.E.M contributed to comparative analysis and phylogeny analysis. J.D., M.C.L., T.Z., and L.W. drafted the manuscript with everyone’s inputs on relevant sections. All authors approved the final manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Crop genetics: lessons from hybrids</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The genetic sequence of a hybrid walnut tree sheds light on the evolution of the parental species and on some important traits. A study led by Ming-Cheng Luo and Jan Dvorak at the University of California Davis, USA, describes a new approach for producing high-quality genome assemblies of the parental species from interspecific hybrids. By applying long-read sequencing technology and optical mapping to a popular hybrid between cultivated Persian/English walnut and a wild relative that is native to North America they were able to completely assemble the genomes of these two species, gain insights into the evolution of their chromosomes and the distribution of disease-resistant genes. This approach could be used on other outcrossing  crops to generate reference-quality genome assemblies and accelerate genetic improvements in commercially important crops.</p></div></section></div></aside>
